<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathophysiology">Pathophysiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathophysiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The heart's inability to pump effectively reduces blood flow, leading to decreased oxygen delivery to tissues. Compensatory mechanisms set up such as heart rate increases (tachycardia) in an attempt to improve output. The sympathetic nervous system is activated leading to peripheral vasoconstriction to maintain blood pressure. Activation of the renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system (RAAS) to retain fluid and increase blood volume.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="symptoms">Symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Early Symptoms: Chest pain or discomfort (often related to a heart attack), Rapid heartbeat (tachycardia), Shortness of breath (dyspnea), Weakness or fatigue</p>
<p>Severe Symptoms: Profound hypotension (very low blood pressure), Cold, clammy skin, Altered mental state (confusion, anxiety), Decreased urine output</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>I. Clinical Assessment: Evaluation of vital signs, physical examination for signs of poor perfusion.
II. Electrocardiogram (ECG): To identify arrhythmias or signs of ischemia.
III. Imaging: Echocardiography may be used to assess heart function and structure.
IV. Laboratory Tests: Cardiac biomarkers (e.g., troponin), complete blood count (CBC), and electrolyte levels.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Stabilization: Ensure airway, breathing, and circulation are adequately supported.</li>
<li>Medications:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Inotropes: To improve heart contractility (e.g., dobutamine).</li>
<li>Vasopressors: To manage low blood pressure (e.g., norepinephrine).</li>
<li>Antiplatelet Agents and Anticoagulants: If the cause is myocardial infarction.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Mechanical Support:</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Intra-aortic Balloon Pump (IABP): To help improve coronary perfusion and cardiac output.</li>
<li>Ventilatory Support: If respiratory distress occurs.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Revascularization: Emergency procedures like angioplasty or coronary artery bypass grafting (CABG) for heart attacks.</li>
<li>Monitoring: Continuous assessment of vital signs, urine output, and response to treatment.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-distributive-shock">3 Distributive Shock:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-distributive-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Results from widespread vasodilation, leading to inadequate blood flow (e.g., septic shock, anaphylactic shock, neurogenic shock).</p>
<p>a) Septic Shock</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>is a severe and potentially fatal condition resulting from sepsis, which is a widespread infection that leads to systemic inflammation and blood flow issues.</p>
<p><strong>Causes:</strong> Typically caused by bacterial infections, but can also arise from fungal or viral infections.</p>
<p><strong>Symptoms:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>☑ High fever or hypothermia</li>
<li>☑ Rapid heart rate and breathing</li>
<li>☑ Confusion or altered mental state</li>
<li>☑ Cold, clammy skin</li>
<li>☑ Low blood pressure</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Management:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>☑ Intravenous fluids</li>
<li>☑ Antibiotics</li>
<li>☑ Vasopressors to maintain blood pressure</li>
<li>☑ Supportive care for organ function</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-anaphylactic-shock">2. Anaphylactic Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-anaphylactic-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>is a severe, life-threatening allergic reaction that occurs rapidly after exposure to an allergen.</p>
<p><strong>Causes:</strong> Common triggers include food allergies (nuts, shellfish), insect stings, medications, and latex.</p>
<p><strong>Symptoms:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>☑ Swelling of the throat and tongue</li>
<li>☑ Difficulty breathing or wheezing</li>
<li>☑ Rapid drop in blood pressure</li>
<li>☑ Skin reactions (hives, flushing)</li>
<li>☑ Abdominal pain, nausea, or vomiting</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Management:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>☑ Immediate administration of epinephrine (adrenaline)</li>
<li>☑ Antihistamines and corticosteroids</li>
<li>☑ Oxygen therapy if needed</li>
<li>☑ Monitoring in a medical facility</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-neurogenic-shock">3. Neurogenic Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-neurogenic-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Neurogenic shock occurs due to the loss of sympathetic nervous system tone, leading to widespread vasodilation and a significant drop in blood pressure.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Causes: Often results from spinal cord injuries, especially those affecting the cervical region, or severe brain injuries.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="symptoms">Symptoms:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Low blood pressure</li>
<li>Warm, dry skin (due to vasodilation)</li>
<li>Bradycardia (slow heart rate)</li>
<li>Weakness or paralysis below the injury site</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Stabilization of the spine if an injury is present</li>
<li>Intravenous fluids and medications to increase blood pressure</li>
<li>Monitoring and supportive care for neurological function</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="obstructive-shock">Obstructive Shock:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#obstructive-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Due to obstruction of blood flow (e.g., pulmonary embolism, cardiac tamponade)</p>
<p>Cause: Physical obstruction of blood flow (e.g., pulmonary embolism, tension pneumothorax, cardiac tamponade).</p>
<p>Characteristics: Impaired blood flow to or from the heart due to mechanical obstruction.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stages-of-shock">Stages of Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stages-of-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="pathophysiology-of-shock">Pathophysiology of Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathophysiology-of-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-initial-stage">i) Initial Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-initial-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Decreased perfusion triggers anaerobic metabolism, leading to lactic acidosis.</li>
<li>Clinical Signs: Often not detectable, as symptoms may not yet be apparent.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="ii-compensatory-stage">ii) Compensatory Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-compensatory-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>The Sympathetic Nervous System is activated leading to Release of catecholamines (e.g., adrenaline) that increase heart rate and vasoconstriction.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The Renin-Angiotensin-Aldosterone System (RAAS) is activated to conserve sodium and water.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Antidiuretic Hormone (ADH) is released to retain fluid and maintain blood volume.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Clinical Signs: Tachycardia, mild hypotension, cool and clammy skin, increased respiratory rate.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="iii-progressive-stage">iii) Progressive Stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-progressive-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Increased Cellular Dysfunction: Continued hypoxia leads to cellular injury and organ dysfunction.</li>
<li>Metabolic Acidosis: Due to accumulation of lactic acid and other metabolic byproducts.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Clinical Signs: Severe hypotension, altered mental status, decreased urine output, and further signs of organ dysfunction.</li>
</ul>
<p>iv) Refractory Stage</p>
<p>Irreversible Cellular Damage: Prolonged shock leads to widespread organ failure and death.</p>
<ul>
<li>Clinical Signs: Severe and persistent hypotension, multi-organ failure, altered consciousness, and eventual cardiac arrest.</li>
<li>Blood Pressure: Profound, unresponsive hypotension; often cannot be stabilized.</li>
<li>Multi-Organ Dysfunction: Severe failure of multiple organ systems (kidneys, liver, lungs), leading to systemic effects.</li>
<li>Severe Acidosis: Extreme metabolic and respiratory acidosis, often leading to irreversible cellular injury.</li>
<li>Neurological Changes: Unresponsiveness, loss of reflexes, and signs of impending death.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="general-management-of-shock">General Management of Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#general-management-of-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The management of shock involves rapid assessment, stabilization, and treatment tailored to the underlying cause.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-initial-assessment">1. Initial Assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-initial-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Vital Signs: Monitor blood pressure, heart rate, respiratory rate, and oxygen saturation.</li>
<li>Physical Examination: Assess for signs of poor perfusion (e.g., cold/clammy skin, altered mental status).</li>
<li>History: Obtain information about symptoms, potential causes, and medical history.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-stabilization">2. Stabilization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-stabilization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Airway, Breathing, Circulation (ABCs):</li>
<li>Ensure the airway is clear and provide supplemental oxygen as needed.</li>
<li>Assess breathing and provide ventilatory support if necessary.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="3-fluid-resuscitation">3. Fluid Resuscitation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-fluid-resuscitation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Crystalloids: Administer intravenous (IV) fluids (e.g., normal saline, lactated Ringer's solution) to restore intravascular volume, especially in hypovolemic shock.</li>
<li>Blood Products: If there is significant hemorrhage, consider transfusions of packed red blood cells (PRBCs) to replace lost volume.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="4-pharmacological-support">4. Pharmacological Support<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-pharmacological-support" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Inotropes: In cases of cardiogenic shock, medications like dobutamine to improve heart contractility.</li>
<li>Vasopressors: Medications like norepinephrine or epinephrine to elevate blood pressure, particularly in septic or distributive shock.</li>
<li>Antibiotics: If septic shock is suspected, initiate broad-spectrum antibiotics promptly.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="5-identify-and-treat-the-underlying-cause">5. Identify and Treat the Underlying Cause<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-identify-and-treat-the-underlying-cause" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Hypovolemic Shock: Control the source of fluid loss (e.g., stop bleeding, treat dehydration).: Administer crystalloids (e.g., normal saline) and blood products if necessary.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Cardiogenic Shock: Treat underlying cardiac issues (e.g., thrombolysis, angioplasty), manage fluid overload, and use inotropes. Revascularization (e.g., angioplasty) may be necessary for myocardial infarction; consider advanced heart failure treatments.</p>
<p>Septic Shock: Administer broad-spectrum antibiotics and address the source of infection. Identify the source of infection (e.g., abscess, pneumonia) and initiate targeted treatment.</p>
<p>Anaphylactic Shock: Administer epinephrine, antihistamines, and corticosteroids.</p>
<p>Neurogenic Shock: Use vasopressors and manage spinal cord injury.</p>
<p>&gt; Obstructive Shock: Treat the obstruction (e.g., relieve tension pneumothorax, remove embolism).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-monitoring">6. Monitoring<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-monitoring" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>&gt; Continuous Vital Signs: Monitor for changes in blood pressure, heart rate, and respiratory status.</p>
<p>&gt; Urine Output: Measure urine output to assess kidney function and fluid status.</p>
<p>&gt; Laboratory Tests: Regularly check blood tests, including electrolytes, lactate levels, and cardiac biomarkers as needed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="general-symptoms-of-shock">General Symptoms of shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#general-symptoms-of-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>I. Altered Mental Status: Confusion, lethargy, or agitation.</p>
<p>II. Skin Changes: Cool, clammy, or pale skin; in septic shock, skin may be warm and flushed.</p>
<p>III. Vital Signs: Tachycardia, hypotension, tachypnea, and reduced urine output.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="specific-symptoms-by-type">Specific Symptoms by Type<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#specific-symptoms-by-type" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>I. Hypovolemic Shock: Signs of bleeding or fluid loss, dry mucous membranes.</p>
<p>II. Cardiogenic Shock: Symptoms of heart failure, such as dyspnea, pulmonary edema, and jugular venous distention.</p>
<p>III. Distributive Shock: Fever (septic shock), rash (anaphylactic shock), or loss of sympathetic tone (neurogenic shock).</p>
<p>IV. Obstructive Shock: Symptoms related to obstruction, such as sudden chest pain (pulmonary embolism) or signs of respiratory distress (tension pneumothorax).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>I. History and Physical Examination: Identify potential causes, assess vital signs, and evaluate the patient's overall condition.</p>
<p>II. Laboratory Tests</p>
<p>☑ Complete Blood Count (CBC): To identify anemia or infection.</p>
<p>☑ Electrolytes and Renal Function Tests: To assess organ function and fluid balance.</p>
<p>☑ Arterial Blood Gas (ABG): To evaluate acid-base status and oxygenation.</p>
<p>iii) Imaging Studies: Chest X-ray, Ultrasound, CT scan: To identify structural causes of shock, such as cardiac tamponade or pulmonary embolism.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="potential-complications">Potential Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#potential-complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>&gt; Multi-Organ Failure: Due to prolonged shock and inadequate tissue perfusion.</p>
<p>&gt; Disseminated Intravascular Coagulation (DIC): Can occur in severe sepsis.</p>
<p>&gt; Acute Respiratory Distress Syndrome (ARDS): Often seen in severe shock states.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="case-study-question-shock">Case Study Question: Shock<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#case-study-question-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Scenario: A 35-year-old male presents to the emergency department after a motorcycle accident. He appears anxious, has a rapid heart rate (tachycardia), and is sweating profusely. His blood pressure is 90/50 mmHg, and he has cold, clammy skin. He reports feeling dizzy and faint.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="questions">Questions:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#questions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>What type of shock is the patient most likely experiencing? Why?</li>
<li>What immediate interventions should be implemented in the emergency department?</li>
<li>What are some potential complications if shock is not promptly treated?</li>
<li>What assessments should be made to evaluate the severity of shock?</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="burns">BURNS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#burns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A burn is an injury that results from direct exposure to any thermal, electrical, chemical, or radiation source.</p>
<p>It occurs when energy from a heat source is transferred into body tissues beyond what the body could hold, leading to tissue injury.</p>
<p>It is characterized by severe skin damage that causes the affected skin cells to die.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-burns">Types of Burns<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-burns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Thermal burns. A thermal burn is a burn to the skin caused by any external heat source like a flame, hot liquids, or hot metals.</li>
<li>Chemical burns. A chemical burn can be caused by many substances, such as strong acids, drain cleaners (lye), paint thinner, and gasoline that touches your skin can cause it to burn.</li>
<li>Radiation burns. A radiation burn is the least common type. Sunburn is a type of radiation burn and other exposure to nuclear radiation, like X-rays or radiation therapy to treat cancer, can also cause these.</li>
<li>Electrical burns. An electrical burn occurs when the skin comes into contact with an electrical current and it passes through the body from faulty electrical wiring.</li>
<li>Friction burns. A friction burn occurs when a hard object rubs off some of the skin. It's both an abrasion or scrape and a heat burn just like motorcycle and bike accidents and a carpet burn.</li>
<li>Cold burns. A cold burn also called “frostbite” occurs when the skin comes into direct contact with something very cold for a prolonged period of time.</li>
<li>Inhalation injury. An inhalation injury is caused by smoke associated with flame injury or inhaled carbon monoxide which is a by-product of incomplete combustion.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Signs and Symptoms of Burn Injury</p>
<ol>
<li>Local Response</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Small Burns (&lt;25%). Response of the body is localized.</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Redness: This occurs due to increased blood flow to the affected area as the body responds to injury. It's a sign of inflammation and part of the healing process.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Swelling: happens when fluid leaks from blood vessels into surrounding tissues. This is another aspect of the inflammatory response, aimed at protecting the area and promoting healing.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pain: arises from the activation of nerve endings in the skin. It serves as a protective mechanism, prompting individuals to avoid further injury.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Blistering: Blisters form in second-degree burns as the body creates a protective layer of fluid between the damaged skin layers. This helps to cushion the area and prevent infection.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Dry or Peeling Skin: In first-degree burns, the outer layer of skin may become dry and peel as it heals.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Change in Skin Texture: Third-degree burns can cause significant damage to the skin's structure, leading to a leathery or waxy appearance. This indicates deeper tissue damage.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Loss of Sensation: In severe burns, third- and fourth-degree, the nerve endings may be destroyed, leading to numbness in the affected area.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Systemic response
Large Burns (&gt;25%). Response of the body is systemic.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>The release of cytokines and other inflammatory mediators at the injury site has a systemic effect once the burn reaches 30% of the total body surface area.</p>
<ul>
<li>Cardiovascular changes. Due to fluid loss from the burn wound, it results in systemic hypotension, increased heart rate, and end organ hypoperfusion.</li>
<li>Hypothermia-inability of the skin to regulate temperature due to loss of skin function.</li>
<li>Respiratory changes. Hyperventilation and increased respiratory rate occur. Inflammatory mediators cause bronchoconstriction, and in severe burns adult respiratory distress syndrome can occur.</li>
<li>Metabolic changes. The basal metabolic rate increases up to three times its original rate. For severely burned patients, the resting metabolic rate at thermal neutral temperature (30°C) tops 140% of predicted basal rate on admission, reduces to 130% once the wounds are fully healed, then to 120% at 6 months after injury.</li>
<li>Immunological changes. Non-specific down regulation of the immune response occurs, affecting both cell mediated and humoral pathways.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Electrolyte imbalances. Hyponatremia and hyperkalemia occur. Hyponatraemia is frequent, and the restoration of sodium losses in the burn tissue is therefore essential. Hyperkalemia is also characteristic during this time because of the massive tissue necrosis. Hyponatraemia (Na) (&lt; 135 mEq/L) is due to extracellular sodium depletion following changes in cellular permeability.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Organ dysfunction—lungs, kidneys.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Psychological responses. Effects on psychological health includes shock, numbness, disbelief, depression, denial, mourning, perceived losses.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-burn-injury">Management of Burn Injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-burn-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Management of burn injury is categorized into three phases of care: emergent phase, acute phase, and rehabilitation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-emergent-phase">1. Emergent Phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-emergent-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The emergent phase starts from the time of burn injury and ends when the patient is hemodynamically stable, capillary permeability has been restored, and fluid resuscitation has been completed. Usually 48-72 hours from the time of injury. The emergent phase is also known as the resuscitative phase, and the goals of this phase include prevention of hypovolemic shock and preservation of vital organ functioning.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-asses-for-the-burn-depth">A) Asses for the burn depth.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-asses-for-the-burn-depth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Burn depth is assessed 24 hours after injury as blisters and other injuries may evolve.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="i-first-degree-burn-superficial-partial-thickness-burn">I. First Degree Burn (Superficial Partial Thickness Burn).<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-first-degree-burn-superficial-partial-thickness-burn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>☑ the skin function remains intact.</li>
<li>☑ affects the epidermis leading to: Erythema, Edema, Pain but without, blisters, Fluid loss is MILD</li>
<li>☑ These burns typically heal within 3 to 5 days and without permanent scarring</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="ii-second-degree-burn-deep-partial-thickness-burn">II. Second Degree Burn (Deep Partial Thickness Burn).<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-second-degree-burn-deep-partial-thickness-burn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>☑ the skin function is lost.</li>
<li>☑ injuries can easily convert to or require the same management as full-thickness.</li>
<li>☑ affects the dermis and epidermis, leading to: Erythema, Edema, Pain with blisters, Pink to reddish skin, Fluid loss is MODERATE</li>
<li>☑ These burns typically heal within 14 to 21 days, depending on the depth</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="iii-third-degree-burn-full-thickness-burn">III. Third-Degree Burn (Full Thickness Burn).<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-third-degree-burn-full-thickness-burn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>☑ skin function is lost,</li>
<li>☑ grafting is required for functional healing.</li>
<li>☑ will almost always require hospital admission.</li>
<li>☑ affects the subcutaneous tissues, epidermis, and dermis</li>
<li>☑ leads to: Pearly white or charred appearance of the skin. Mottled brown, black, or red burn site, Pain is absent, Fluid loss is SEVERE</li>
<li>☑ requires medical imterevention</li>
<li>☑ may need skin graft</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5 studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="iv-fourth-degree-burn-deep-fullness-thickness-burn">IV. Fourth-Degree Burn (Deep Fullness Thickness Burn).<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iv-fourth-degree-burn-deep-fullness-thickness-burn" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>☑ the affected areas go through both layers of the skin and underlying tissue as well as deeper tissue.
☑ involves muscle and bone.
☑ Burned part is black/charred
☑ Fluid loss is VERY SEVERE
☑ Requires surgical intervention
☑ May lead to disability.
☑ These injuries are the deepest and require excision, possibly amputation of extremities, and skin grafting to heal.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-ea1dd584.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-04ebd5f5.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-92c4bcf1.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg"></p>
<p>&gt; Assess the burn size and extent.</p>
<p>The extent of the burn wound is reported as a percent of the TBSA injured.</p>
<p>In adults, this is easily estimated by using the rule of nines.</p>
<p>In children A more accurate assessment tool is the Lund-Browder chart which is more detailed and accounts for changes in body areas according to age.</p>
<p>This chart may be used for both children and adults and is frequently used in critical and acute care settings</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Rule of Nines. A common method, the rule of nines is a quick way to estimate the extent of burns in adults through dividing the body into multiples of nine and the sum total of these parts is equal to the total body surface area injured.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Rule of Nines is common for burns over large surface areas (Figure 8.17). The head and each arm represent 9 percent each of TBSA. The chest and abdomen, entire back, and each leg are 18 percent each. The perineal area is 1 percent. Thus, a patient whose entire right arm and back were burned would have a TBSA of 27 percent.</p>
</li>
</ul>

<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-c653724f.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg"></p>
<p>Figure 8.17 Using this image, it is easy to calculate the burn percentage for a patient. (attribution: Copyright Rice University, OpenStax, under CC BY 4.0 license)</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Lund and Browder Method. This method recognizes the percentage of surface area of various anatomic parts, especially the head and the legs, as it relates to the age of the patient.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lund-browder-chart">Lund &amp; Browder Chart<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lund-browder-chart" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><a href="http://www.ThePlasticsFella.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.ThePlasticsFella.com</a></p>
<ol>
<li>Mark the areas of the burn</li>
<li>Calculate area using the age-table below</li>
<li>Do not include superficial burns</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Age</th><th></th><th>0</th><th>1</th><th>5</th><th>10</th><th>15</th><th>Adult</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Front or back half</td><td></td><td>(%)</td><td>(%)</td><td>(%)</td><td>(%)</td><td>(%)</td><td>(%)</td></tr><tr><td>A</td><td>(Head)</td><td>9½</td><td>8½</td><td>6½</td><td>5½</td><td>4½</td><td>3½</td></tr><tr><td>B</td><td>(Thigh)</td><td>2½</td><td>3½</td><td>4</td><td>4½</td><td>4½</td><td>4½</td></tr><tr><td>C</td><td>(Leg)</td><td>2½</td><td>2½</td><td>2½</td><td>3</td><td>3½</td><td>3½</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251224-9a1e9eee.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg"></p>
<ul>
<li>Palmer Method. The size of the patient's palm, not including the surface area of the digits, is approximately 1% of the TBSA, and the patient's palm without the fingers is equivalent to 0.5% TBSA and serves as a general measurement for all age groups.</li>
<li>Small Burns (&lt;25%). Response of the body is localized.</li>
<li>Large Burns (&gt;25%). Response of the body is systemic.</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; B) Assess for the burn location.</p>
<p>The area of a burn injury usually directs treatment. Burns on the face, hands, feet, and genitalia, as well as large burns in other areas of the body and those associated with inhalation injury, are often referred to burn centers for specialized expertise.</p>
<ul>
<li>Head, Neck, and Chest. Respiratory</li>
<li>Face. Corneal ulceration</li>
<li>Perineum. Contaminated with urine and feces</li>
<li>Circumferential Burns of Extremities. Compromise circulation</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; C) Airway Management</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
