<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">✓</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\checkmark</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6922em;"></span><span class="mord amsrm">✓</span></span></span></span></span> Airway Management is vital to maintain the airway and provide supplemental oxygen in patients with major burns. Airway management is crucial for types of burns related to inhalation injury.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">✓</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\checkmark</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6922em;"></span><span class="mord amsrm">✓</span></span></span></span></span> Oxygenation: CO2 poisoning. 100% of oxygen is delivered via a tight fitting non-rebreather mask until carbon monoxide falls to 15%.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">✓</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\checkmark</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6922em;"></span><span class="mord amsrm">✓</span></span></span></span></span> Mechanical ventilator as indicated.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">✓</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\checkmark</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6922em;"></span><span class="mord amsrm">✓</span></span></span></span></span> Endotracheal suctioning.</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">✓</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\checkmark</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6922em;"></span><span class="mord amsrm">✓</span></span></span></span></span> Head of the bed is elevated to facilitate maximum expansion of the lungs.</p>
<p>&gt; D) Fluid Resuscitation</p>
<p>Fluid Resuscitation refers to replacing fluids in burn patients to prevent hypovolemia and hypoperfusion that can result from the body’s systemic response to burn injury.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Initiate fluid administration. Peripheral IV access may initially be used though in larger and more severe cases of burns, a central venous access is recommended as a large volume of fluid is required.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Use American Burn Association (ABA) guidelines for fluid resuscitation. (parkland’s formula) The formula for the total fluid requirement in 24 hours is as follows: 4ml x TBSA (%) x body weight (kg). [Example: Patient weighs 80 kg with TSBA of 20% = 4mL x 80 kg = 320 x 20 = 6,400 mL]</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>First half of the solution is given in the first 8 hours (3,200 mL)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>One quarter of the solution is given in the second 8 hours (1,600 mL)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Another quarter of the solution is given in the third 8 hours (1,600 mL)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Avoid colloid-containing solution for the first 24 hours because it may aggravate edema due to an increase in capillary permeability.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The amount of fluid in the second 24 hours will depend on the patient’s urine output and hemodynamic studies (Hct, CVP, and BUN/Crea)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Colloid-containing solutions may be given with D5W with glucose.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Monitor urine output. A urine output of 0.5 to 1 mL/kg/h is used as an indication of appropriate resuscitation in thermal and chemical injuries. In electrical injuries, a urine output of 75 to 100 mL/h is the goal.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; E) Diet</p>
<p>The larger the burn size, the more nutrients are needed for healing.</p>
<ul>
<li>Provide additional calories. Patients need more calories than normal when they’re recovering from a burn injury. That’s why nutrition is a major component of burn treatment. A diet high in calories and protein supports the immune system to decrease risk of infection; helps wounds heal faster; maintains muscle mass; and minimizes weight loss to support rehabilitation.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; F) Pain Management</p>
<p>Pain due to burns can range from mild to severe to excruciating. Pain management, which includes pharmacologic and nonpharmacologic approaches, is a central component of the complex issues involved in treating patients with burns.</p>
<ul>
<li>NO intramuscular or subcutaneous administration because the patient is hypovolemic.</li>
<li>Intravenous analgesics: Morphine, Demerol</li>
<li>Oral administration is NOT considered due to GI dysfunction.</li>
<li>Minor burns: per oral</li>
<li>Nonpharmacological: Deep breathing exercises, guided imagery</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; G) Wound Care</p>
<p>Prescribed topical agents are administered before the wound is covered with layers of dry dressings.</p>
<ul>
<li>Use ointments. Antibiotic ointments or creams are used to fight or treat infections in patients with second-degree burns. Using these ointments may require the use of bandages.</li>
<li>Regularly change dressings. Dressings may need to be changed regularly. The skin and the burn wound should be washed gently with mild soap and rinsed well with tap water/ normal saline. Use a soft wash cloth or piece of gauze to gently remove old medications.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Acute Phase</li>
</ol>
<p>The acute phase of burn management starts 48-72 hours from the burn injury when the patient is hemodynamically stable with completed fluid resuscitation and restored capillary permeability and ends upon wound closure.</p>
<p>i) Prevent infection. Patients with burns are at the highest risk for healthcare-associated infections (HAIs). The loss of the skin’s barrier function, combined with necrotic tissue, produces an environment conducive to bacterial growth. Nursing interventions to prevent infection includes:</p>
<p>☑ Watch out for signs of infection. Erythema, warmth, malodorous exudates, and tenderness.
☑ Initiate universal precaution. Use of gowns, gloves, and eye protection. Including frequent hand hygiene.
☑ Wound culture and antimicrobial therapy. Culture and sensitivity is usually ordered on admission for patients with burns to test for presence of MRSA.
☑ Wound care. Early excision and closure of the burn wound helps in preventing infection.
☑ Control of hyperglycemia. Insulin is indicated (even without diabetes) for severely burned patients to improve protein synthesis, attenuate lean body mass loss, decrease hypermetabolism, and accelerate donor healing time.</p>
<p>This document is available on
5 studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>ii) Provide nutritional support. Nutritional support through total parenteral nutrition or enteral tube feeding for patients with burns is aggressive. There should be an increase in calories, proteins, and fats.
iii) Provide proper wound care. Wound cleansing should be done through use of normal saline</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="wound-care">WOUND CARE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#wound-care" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-wound-cleansing">I. Wound Cleansing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-wound-cleansing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Involves immersion, spray, or showering 30 minutes or less.</li>
<li>More than 30 minutes can cause heat loss, pain, and stress.</li>
<li>Analgesics before the procedure.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ii-debridement">II. Debridement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-debridement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Debridement is the removal of necrotic tissues to prevent bacterial growth-promoting wound healing.</p>
<ul>
<li>Mechanical Debridement. Involves the use of forceps and scissors to trim away loose necrotic tissues.</li>
<li>Enzymatic Debridement. Involves the use of proteolytic or fibriolytic enzymes to digest necrotic tissues.</li>
<li>Surgical Debridement. Involves excision of loose necrotic tissues.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iii-antimicrobial-agents-or-ointments">III. Antimicrobial Agents or Ointments<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-antimicrobial-agents-or-ointments" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Silver Sulfadiazine. Once or twice daily.</li>
<li>Open Method. The wound is left exposed to air after application.</li>
<li>Close method. Sterile gauze is impregnated.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="iv-surgical-management">IV. Surgical Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iv-surgical-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Autografting.-Autografting is the surgical removal of a superficial layer of the patient’s own unburned skin (donor site) which is subsequently grafted to the patient’s excised open wound.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="post-op-considerations">Post Op Considerations:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#post-op-considerations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Promote graft adherence through immobilization</li>
<li>Bed rest for 10 days</li>
<li>Keep graft site free from pressure</li>
<li>Avoid weight-bearing activities</li>
<li>To remove exudate, roll a cotton tip applicator</li>
<li>Watch out for foul smelling discharge. It may indicate infection.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>As prescribed, small amount of <strong>blood</strong> may be removed beneath the grafted skin by rolling gauze from the center to the periphery where the blood can be absorbed by the sterile gauze.</li>
<li>Aspirate if with large amount of blood using a small gauge needle as prescribed by the physician.</li>
<li>Apply <strong>cocoa butter/petroleum jelly</strong> to prevent dryness.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-rehabilitation-phase">3. Rehabilitation Phase<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-rehabilitation-phase" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The rehabilitation phase occurs immediately after the burn has occurred and can extend for years after the initial injury.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Minimize functional loss.</strong> Burn rehabilitation includes rehabilitation programs to help return the patients to their highest level of function within the content of their injuries.</li>
<li>Provide psychosocial support. Includes counseling, patient teaching, and help the patient reintegrate with society through various programs aimed at burn survivors.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="monitoring-and-managing-potential-complications">Monitoring and Managing Potential Complications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#monitoring-and-managing-potential-complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-acute-respiratory-failure">1. Acute respiratory failure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-acute-respiratory-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Acute respiratory failure can occur in burn patients for several reasons:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="a-inhalation-injury">a. Inhalation Injury:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-inhalation-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>II. Burns to the face or neck, or exposure to smoke and toxic gases, can lead to inhalation injuries. This can cause airway swelling, obstruction, and damage to lung tissue, resulting in respiratory failure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="a-fluid-resuscitation">a. Fluid Resuscitation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-fluid-resuscitation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>III. Large volumes of fluids given for burn resuscitation can lead to pulmonary edema (fluid accumulation in the lungs), impairing gas exchange and contributing to respiratory failure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="a-infection">a. Infection:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>IV. Burn patients are at high risk for infections, including pneumonia. Infection can lead to increased inflammation and fluid accumulation in the lungs, worsening respiratory function.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="a-reduced-mobility">a. Reduced Mobility:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-reduced-mobility" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>V. Severe burns often require prolonged immobilization, which can lead to atelectasis (collapse of lung tissue) and reduced lung expansion, increasing the risk of respiratory failure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="a-sepsis">a. Sepsis:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-sepsis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>VI. Systemic infection or sepsis can cause widespread inflammation, leading to acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS) and respiratory failure.</p>
<p>VII. Pain and Sedation:</p>
<p>Severe pain can inhibit deep breathing, and sedative medications may reduce respiratory drive, both of which can contribute to respiratory complications.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>S</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Assess for increasing dyspnea, stridor, changes in respiratory patterns; monitor pulse oximetry and ABG values to detect problematic oxygen saturation and increasing CO2; monitor chest x-rays; assess for cerebral hypoxia (eg, restlessness, confusion); report deteriorating respiratory status immediately to physician; and assist as needed with intubation or escharotomy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-distributive-shock">2. Distributive shock:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-distributive-shock" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>refers to a state where there is inadequate blood flow to tissues due to widespread vasodilation and decreased vascular resistance.</p>
<p>burns can lead to this condition through:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-fluid-loss">a. Fluid Loss:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-fluid-loss" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>II. Severe burns result in significant fluid loss through damaged skin. This loss can lead to hypovolemia (low blood volume), prompting the body to compensate by dilating blood vessels.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-vasodilation">a. Vasodilation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-vasodilation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>III. The inflammatory response to burn injury triggers the release of various mediators (like histamines and cytokines) that cause vasodilation. This results in increased blood vessel diameter and decreases systemic vascular resistance, contributing to distributive shock.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-sepsis-risk">a. Sepsis Risk:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-sepsis-risk" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>IV. Burn injuries increase the risk of infection and sepsis. Sepsis can cause further vasodilation and increased capillary permeability, leading to a state of distributive shock as blood flow is poorly distributed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-hypotension">a. Hypotension:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-hypotension" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>V. The combination of fluid loss, vasodilation, and potential heart strain can lead to hypotension (low blood pressure), which is a hallmark of distributive shock</p>
<p>Monitor for early signs of shock (decreased urine output, cardiac output, pulmonary artery pressure, pulmonary capillary wedge pressure, blood pressure, or increasing pulse) or progressive edema. Administer fluid resuscitation as ordered in response to physical findings; continue monitoring fluid status.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-acute-renal-failure">3. Acute renal failure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-acute-renal-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Also known as acute kidney injury (AKI), is a sudden decline in kidney function, leading to the accumulation of waste products in the blood and an imbalance of fluids and electrolytes</p>
<p>Burns can lead to acute renal failure (ARF) due to several interrelated factors such as:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-fluid-loss-and-hypovolemia">a. Fluid Loss and Hypovolemia:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-fluid-loss-and-hypovolemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>II. Severe burns can cause significant fluid loss through damaged skin. This can lead to hypovolemia (low blood volume), resulting in decreased blood flow to the kidneys and pre-renal ARF.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-2-myoglobinuria">a. 2. Myoglobinuria:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-2-myoglobinuria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>III. In cases of severe burns, muscle tissue can be damaged, leading to the release of myoglobin into the bloodstream. Myoglobin can be harmful to the kidneys, potentially causing intrinsic renal injury and contributing to ARF.</p>
<p>a. 3. Sepsis:</p>
<p>IV. Burn injuries increase the risk of infection and sepsis, which can cause systemic inflammatory responses that may lead to renal impairment.</p>
<p>a. 4. Electrolyte Imbalance:</p>
<p>V. Burns can disrupt the balance of electrolytes in the body, such as potassium and sodium, which can affect kidney function and contribute to ARF.</p>
<p>VI. Renal Ischemia: The combination of fluid loss, inflammation, and systemic shock can lead to renal ischemia (insufficient blood flow to the kidneys), further increasing the risk of kidney damage</p>
<p>VII. Monitor and report abnormal urine output and quality, blood urea nitrogen (BUN) and creatinine levels; assess for urine hemoglobin or myoglobin; administer increased fluids as prescribed.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Compartment syndrome:</li>
</ol>
<p>Compartment syndrome is a serious condition that occurs when there is increased pressure within a closed anatomical space (compartment), leading to decreased blood flow and potential damage to muscles and nerves.</p>
<p>This can result from factors like trauma, swelling, or bleeding.</p>
<p>Symptoms include severe pain, swelling, and a feeling of tightness in the affected area, as well as decreased range of motion and numbness.</p>
<p>If not treated promptly, compartment syndrome can lead to permanent muscle and nerve damage.</p>
<p>Treatment often involves surgical intervention to relieve the pressure</p>
<ul>
<li>Assess peripheral pulses hourly with Doppler; assess neurovascular status of extremities hourly (warmth, capillary refill, sensation, and movement); remove blood pressure cuff after each reading; elevate burned extremities; report any extremity pain, loss of peripheral pulses or sensation; prepare to assist with escharotomies.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="5">
<li>Paralytic ileus:</li>
</ol>
<p>Paralytic ileus is a condition characterized by a temporary cessation of normal bowel movements due to a lack of intestinal motility.</p>
<p>This can lead to a buildup of gas and fluids in the intestines, causing abdominal distension, pain, nausea, and vomiting.</p>
<p>Burns can lead to paralytic ileus for several reasons:</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>I. Fluid Loss: Severe burns cause significant fluid loss, leading to dehydration and electrolyte imbalances. These changes can affect intestinal motility.
II. Stress Response: The body's stress response to severe burns can release hormones and inflammatory mediators that may slow down gut motility.
III. Pain and Opioids: Pain management often involves opioids, which can reduce intestinal peristalsis and contribute to ileus.
IV. Surgery and Immobilization: If burn treatment involves surgical intervention, postoperative factors, such as reduced mobility and anesthesia effects, can further contribute to the development of paralytic ileus</p>
<p>Maintain nasogastric tube on low intermittent suction until bowel sounds resume; auscultate abdomen regularly for distention and bowel sounds.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-curling-s-ulcer">6. Curling's ulcer:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-curling-s-ulcer" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Curling's ulcer is a type of stress-induced gastric ulcer that can occur in patients with severe burns or major trauma.</p>
<p>It is triggered by the physiological stress of severe burns, leading to decreased blood flow to the stomach and increased acid production.</p>
<p>It forms in the duodenum (the first part of the small intestine) but can also occur in the stomach.</p>
<p>Symptoms: abdominal pain, nausea, vomiting, and signs of gastrointestinal bleeding, such as melena (black, tarry stools) or hematemesis (vomiting blood)</p>
<p>Assess gastric aspirate for blood and pH; assess stools for occult blood; administer antacids and histamine blockers (eg, ranitidine [Zantac]) as prescribed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="revision-quotations">REVISION QUOTATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#revision-quotations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>A 32-year-old client, who is on an anticoagulant medication due to a history of deep vein thrombosis, arrives at the emergency department after accidentally spilling a pot of boiling water mixed with hot oil on their right arm while attempting to deep-fry food at home. The client is in significant pain and reports that the burn occurred approximately 30 minutes ago. Upon assessment, the nurse observes a large burned area on the client's right arm with a mix of pink and mottled red areas, blisters, and signs of active bleeding from the blistered areas. The burned area is very painful to touch and the client is visibly anxious.</li>
</ol>
<p>Based on the assessment findings and the client's medical history, how will the nurse categorize this burn injury?</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>The client has burns on both legs. These areas appear white and leather-like. No blisters or bleeding are present, and there is just a "small amount of pain." How will the nurse categorize this injury?</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:pngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">pngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Nine year old Kenny is brought to the emergency department after his clothes caught on fire while he and his younger brothers were playing with matches in the family garage. He has partial thickness and full thickness burns on the anterior chest, anterior abdomen, shoulders, entire upper right arm, and entire right hand. There is singed nasal hair apparent on physical examination and some minor burns on his face. Kenny's height is 45 inches and weight is 40 pounds. A foley catheter is inserted and a small amount of urine is obtained. Two IV routes are established for fluid replacement. Morphine sulfate 2 mg IV is ordered for pain. Kenny is stabilized and transferred to the burn unit. He receives daily hydrotherapy &amp; debridement, dressing changes twice a day, and is placed on prophylactic antibiotics.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Describe the difference between partial thickness and full thickness burns, including healing times, tissue involved, and the appearance of burn injury.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Calculate the extent of Kenny's injury utilizing the modified rule of 9's.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>(Kenny's injury involves 26.5% of his body. 13% comes from the burn involving his anterior chest and abdomen. 8% comes from the burn involving his shoulder and upper right arm. 3% comes from the burn involving his right lower arm, and 2.5% comes from the burn on his right hand.)</p>
<ol start="9">
<li>Describe emergency care for Kenny.</li>
